The Permissibility of Describing Allah with Non-Revealed Terms
Author argues for permissibility of describing Allah with 'al-Qadim' and criticizes Ibn Baz's obstinacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that it is permissible to describe Allah with terms not necessarily mentioned in revelation, provided they are not names, and that Ibn Baz's objection to 'al-Qadim' as a description is invalid.

Core Idea
Describing Allah with 'al-Qadim' is permissible even if not a revealed name.

The editor concludes the discussion by addressing the distinction between names and descriptions. He argues that even if 'al-Qadim' were not a revealed name, it is permissible as a description. He criticizes Ibn Baz for failing to consider this distinction and accuses him of obstinate accusation-mongering.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'علماً أنه يجوز' to the end along with the translation. This section focuses on the permissibility of descriptions.
2
Understand the name vs description distinction
Notice that the author accepts that names are tawqifiyya (revealed), but descriptions are not necessarily so. This is a key theological principle.
3
Analyze the author's rhetorical question
The author asks why Ibn Baz did not consider 'al-Qadim' as a description rather than a name. This shows that Ibn Baz's objection is based on a misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the final accusation
Consider the author's claim that Ibn Baz and his ilk are obstinate in attaching accusations. How does this fit into the overall polemic?

Self-Subsistence: Definition and Refutation of Hulul and Ittihad
Sanusi defines qiyam bil-nafs and refutes hulul and ittihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of Qiyam bil-Nafs as the negation of need for a locus and specificator, and why the beliefs of incarnation (hulul) and union (ittihad) are incompatible with it.

Core Idea
God's self-subsistence means He needs no place and no maker—contrary to false creeds.

Sanusi continues his enumeration of divine attributes, now focusing on God's absolute independence. He defines qiyam bil-nafs and immediately warns against two heresies that compromise it: hulul (incarnation) and ittihad (union). This lesson unpacks the definition and shows why these false doctrines are rejected by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening line where Sanusi defines qiyam bil-nafs as negation of need for a mahall and mukhasis.
This is the core definition.
2
Understand the contrast with hulul
See how the definition rules out any indwelling (hulul) as claimed by Christians and Batinites.
The consensus is that such belief is kufr.
3
Understand the contrast with ittihad
Note the additional refutation of ittihad (union), where two things become one.
This is also kufr.
4
Grasp the implication
Sanusi adds that God needs no specificator, otherwise He would be originated.
This reinforces the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة قيامه تعالى بنفسه. وحقيقة القيام بالنفس عبارة عن انتفاء الاحتياج إلى المحل والمخصص. فهو سبحانه لا يحتاج إلى ذات يحل فيها كما تحل الصفة في الموصوف كما تدعيه النصارى والباطنية. وقد أجمع على أن من يعتقد أن الله تعالى في شيء فهو كافر ، وأيضاً مثل الحلول والاتحاد وهو صيرورة الشيئين شيئاً واحداً. والقول بالاتحاد كفر أيضاً ، قال صاحب إضاءة الدجنة في عقيدة أهل السنة. ولا تصخ لمذهب النصارى أو من إلى دعوى حلول صارا وذاك كالقول بالاتحاد نحلة أهل الزيغ والإلحاد وهو سبحانه لا يحتاج إلى مخصص، فلو احتاج كان حادثاً. والدليل على أن الله تعالى غني عن المحل والمخصص من الكتاب قوله تعالى ( يَأَيُّهَا النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ وَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْغَنِيُّ الْحَمِيدُ ) (فاطر: ١٥)، ومن السنة قوله : "اللهم أنت لا إله إلا أنت الغني . وقد أجمعت الأمة على أن الله تعالى قائم بنفسه أي غني. وأما برهان العقل فسيأتي.
The fourth: His self-subsistence. The reality of self-subsistence is an expression of the negation of need for a locus and a specificator. He, exalted, does not need an essence to dwell in, as an attribute dwells in the described, as the Christians and Batinites claim. And there is consensus that whoever believes that God exists in something is a disbeliever, and also [beliefs] like incarnation and union, which is the transformation of two things into one thing. Saying union is also disbelief. The author of Iḍā’at ad-Dujna said in the creed of the Ahl as-Sunna: 'And do not listen to the school of the Christians or those who claim incarnation became [?] and that is like the saying of union, the party of deviation and heresy.' And He, exalted, does not need a specificator; for if He needed [one], He would be originated. The proof that God is free of need for a locus and a specificator from the Book is His saying: 'O mankind, you are the needy to Allah, and Allah is the Free of need, the Praiseworthy' (Fatir: 15). And from the Sunnah is His saying: 'O Allah, You are the only deity, You are the Free of need.' And the community has agreed that God is self-subsistent, i.e., free of need. As for the rational proof, it will follow.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth divine attribute in Sanusi's list: qiyamuhu binafsihi (self-subsistence). It opens by stating the definition: it is the negation of need for a locus (mahall) and a specificator (mukhasis). The author clarifies that God does not require an essence in which to dwell, as an attribute dwells in its subject, a claim made by Christians (incarnation) and Batini sects (hulul). He then states the grave ruling: consensus holds that believing God exists within something is disbelief. Similarly, the belief in union (ittihad), where two distinct things become one, is also disbelief. He quotes a poetic couplet from the work Iḍā’at ad-Dujna that warns against such deviant sects. Next, he re-emphasizes that God needs no specificator, as needing one would imply origination (hawadath). He then cites textual proof: from the Quran, verse 35:15 declaring God as al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient); from the Sunnah, a short hadith addressing God as al-Ghani; and from Ijma (consensus) that God is qā’im binafsih, meaning ghani. He concludes by noting that the rational proof will be presented later, indicating this is only a textual proof section.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
المحل
المخصص
الحلول
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'qiyam bil-nafs' implies God needs a self-subsistent essence like an independent object.
Correction: It simply negates need for a locus and specificator; God is not a substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'qiyam bi al-nafs'?
A) God is an independent substance that dwells in His creation.
B) God has no need for a locus (mahall) or a specificator (mukhasis).
C) God is united with His creation.
D) God needs a specificator but not a locus.

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence negates any need for a dwelling or a creator, making hulul and ittihad clear violations of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously imagine God as being 'in' the sky or 'in' a particular place? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what must you do to correct your mental image of God?
?
If you ever felt that you need to be in a 'holy place' to be close to God, how might this lesson reveal a misunderstanding of God's self-subsistence? How will you change your approach to seeking closeness to Him?
?
When you hear someone say 'God is with us' in a physical sense, how would you respond using the definition of self-subsistence from this lesson? What would you be willing to risk to correct such a belief?

Textual Proofs for God's Self-Subsistence
Sanusi cites textual proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma for self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and consensus-based proofs that establish God's freedom from need for a locus and specificator.

Core Idea
The Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma all affirm that Allah is al-Ghani, the Self-Sufficient.

After stating the definition and refuting false beliefs, Sanusi provides the textual evidence for God's independence. He cites a Quranic verse, a prophetic supplication, and the consensus of the ummah, while promising a rational proof later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs
Notice that Sanusi cites three sources: Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.
These are the 'textual' proofs.
2
Read the Quranic verse
Read verse 35:15, which calls people poor and God al-Ghani.
Ghani means free of need.
3
Read the Hadith
Read the short hadith where the Prophet addresses Allah as al-Ghani.
This prayer is found in Sunan.
4
Note the Ijma
The ummah agrees that God is qā’im binafsihi, i.e., ghani.
Consensus is a proof.
5
Look forward to rational proof
Sanusi promises a rational proof will follow, showing this is only the beginning.
Rational proof is separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة قيامه تعالى بنفسه. وحقيقة القيام بالنفس عبارة عن انتفاء الاحتياج إلى المحل والمخصص. فهو سبحانه لا يحتاج إلى ذات يحل فيها كما تحل الصفة في الموصوف كما تدعيه النصارى والباطنية. وقد أجمع على أن من يعتقد أن الله تعالى في شيء فهو كافر ، وأيضاً مثل الحلول والاتحاد وهو صيرورة الشيئين شيئاً واحداً. والقول بالاتحاد كفر أيضاً ، قال صاحب إضاءة الدجنة في عقيدة أهل السنة. ولا تصخ لمذهب النصارى أو من إلى دعوى حلول صارا وذاك كالقول بالاتحاد نحلة أهل الزيغ والإلحاد وهو سبحانه لا يحتاج إلى مخصص، فلو احتاج كان حادثاً. والدليل على أن الله تعالى غني عن المحل والمخصص من الكتاب قوله تعالى ( يَأَيُّهَا النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ وَاللَّهُ هُوَ الْغَنِيُّ الْحَمِيدُ ) (فاطر: ١٥)، ومن السنة قوله : "اللهم أنت لا إله إلا أنت الغني . وقد أجمعت الأمة على أن الله تعالى قائم بنفسه أي غني. وأما برهان العقل فسيأتي.
The fourth: His self-subsistence. The reality of self-subsistence is an expression of the negation of need for a locus and a specificator. He, exalted, does not need an essence to dwell in, as an attribute dwells in the described, as the Christians and Batinites claim. And there is consensus that whoever believes that God exists in something is a disbeliever, and also [beliefs] like incarnation and union, which is the transformation of two things into one thing. Saying union is also disbelief. The author of Iḍā’at ad-Dujna said in the creed of the Ahl as-Sunna: 'And do not listen to the school of the Christians or those who claim incarnation became [?] and that is like the saying of union, the party of deviation and heresy.' And He, exalted, does not need a specificator; for if He needed [one], He would be originated. The proof that God is free of need for a locus and a specificator from the Book is His saying: 'O mankind, you are the needy to Allah, and Allah is the Free of need, the Praiseworthy' (Fatir: 15). And from the Sunnah is His saying: 'O Allah, You are the only deity, You are the Free of need.' And the community has agreed that God is self-subsistent, i.e., free of need. As for the rational proof, it will follow.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth divine attribute in Sanusi's list: qiyamuhu binafsihi (self-subsistence). It opens by stating the definition: it is the negation of need for a locus (mahall) and a specificator (mukhasis). The author clarifies that God does not require an essence in which to dwell, as an attribute dwells in its subject, a claim made by Christians (incarnation) and Batini sects (hulul). He then states the grave ruling: consensus holds that believing God exists within something is disbelief. Similarly, the belief in union (ittihad), where two distinct things become one, is also disbelief. He quotes a poetic couplet from the work Iḍā’at ad-Dujna that warns against such deviant sects. Next, he re-emphasizes that God needs no specificator, as needing one would imply origination (hawadath). He then cites textual proof: from the Quran, verse 35:15 declaring God as al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient); from the Sunnah, a short hadith addressing God as al-Ghani; and from Ijma (consensus) that God is qā’im binafsih, meaning ghani. He concludes by noting that the rational proof will be presented later, indicating this is only a textual proof section.

Key Terms
الغني
المحل
المخصص
برهان العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Rational proof
B) Quran 35:15
C) Hadith 'اللهم أنت لا إله إلا أنت الغني'
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The textual evidence from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma solidifies the doctrine of God's self-subsistence as free of all need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Meaning of al-Mukhasis and Its Impossibility for God
Footnote defines 'al-mukhasis' and proves its impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'al-mukhasis' (specificator) and why it is impossible to attribute such a need to God.

Core Idea
A specificator is needed only for what can cease to exist; God never can.

This lesson unpacks a brief but crucial footnote from the commentary. It defines the term 'al-mukhasis' — an agent that brings something into existence by preferring existence over non-existence — and then explains why this concept cannot apply to Allah. Understanding this helps clarify the difference between contingent beings and the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own definition and argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the footnote.
2
Define 'mukhasis' in your own words
Based on the passage, write a one-sentence definition of 'mukhasis' that includes its role as an agent who specifies existence over non-existence.
The passage explicitly says 'an agent and bestower of existence'.
3
Understand why it is impossible for God
Identify the key premise: only beings that accept non-existence need a specificator. Then see how the passage applies that premise to God, who does not accept non-existence in essence or attributes.
The logic is a simple syllogism: 1) Only those who accept non-existence need a specificator. 2) God does not accept non-existence. Therefore, God does not need a specificator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالمخصص أي فاعل وموجد يخصصه بالوجود بدلاً عن العدم، فهذا مستحيل في حق الإله، لأن الذي يحتاج إلى المخصص من يقبل العدم، والله تعالى لا يقبله لا في ذاته ولا في صفة من صفاته.
What is meant by 'specificator' is an agent and bestower of existence who specifies it with existence instead of non-existence. This is impossible with respect to the divine, because that which needs a specificator is that which accepts non-existence, whereas God, exalted, does not accept non-existence, neither in His essence nor in any of His attributes.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a footnote clarifying the concept of al-mukhasis (specificator). It explains that a specificator is an agent that brings something into existence, selecting existence over non-existence. The author then argues that such a need for a specificator is impossible for God, because the need for a specificator arises only for beings that can possibly not exist (i.e., that accept non-existence). God, however, cannot be subject to non-existence, neither in His essence nor in any of His attributes. Therefore, God is free from the need for a specificator.

Key Terms
المخصص
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'mukhasis' might mean a prior arbitrary selector; the passage emphasizes it is an agent of existence needed only by contingent beings.
Correction: A mukhasis is specifically an agent that brings existence, not just any arbitrary selector. And it is only needed by things that could not exist, not by God.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is the specificator of all things.
B) Because God does not accept non-existence in His essence or attributes.
C) Because the specificator is only for created things.
D) Because God is not subject to time.

Key Takeaway
God does not need a specificator because He cannot be subject to non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that no attribute of God can ever cease, how would that transform the way you rely on His forgiveness, His provision, and His guidance? What step will you take today to depend on Him as the Eternal One who never fails?

Four Categories of Existing Things
Classifies existents into four types based on need for locus and specificator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of existents in Islamic theology based on their need for a locus (mahall) and a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ), and the unique position of God's attributes.

Core Idea
All existents fall into four categories by need for a locus and a specificator.

This passage from Sanusi's text provides a concise yet profound classification of all existing things. By examining each category's relationship to a locus (mahall) and a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ), we gain clarity on the nature of God, His attributes, and the created world. Let's walk through the classification and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the four categories explicitly listed.
The passage begins with a note (ملاحظة) signaling an important clarification.
2
Identify the two criteria
The classification uses two criteria: need for a locus (محل) and need for a specificator (مخصص). Understand each term.
The specificator is that which brings the thing into existence or determines its nature.
3
List the four categories
Write down the four categories as given: God's Essence, God's attributes, accidents, and bodies. For each, note whether it needs a locus and/or specificator.
Use the Arabic terms: ذات الله, صفات الله, العرض, الجرم.
4
Reflect on the clarification about attributes
The passage adds that God's attributes are not 'in need' of the Essence but 'subsist' in it. This avoids implying deficiency in God.
The attributes are neither identical to nor other than the Essence—a key Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : اعلم أن الموجودات على أربعة أقسام موجود غني عن المحل وهو ذات الله، وموجود غني عن المخصص قائم بالمحل وهو صفات الله، لأن المحل هو الذات وموجود مفتقر إلى المحل والمخصص وهو العرض، لأنه مفتقر إلى القيام بالجرم ، وموجود غني عن المحل مفتقر إلى المخصص وهو الجرم لأنه لا يحتاج إلى ذات يقوم بها ويحتاج إلى الفاعل، لأن الموجد له هو الله. واعلم أيضاً أن صفات الله لا يقال فيها مفتقرة إلى الذات بل يقال قائمة بالذات، لأن الافتقار إنما يكون إلى الغير، ولا يقال إن الصفات غير الذات، كما لا يقال إنها عين الذات.
Note: Know that existing things are of four categories: An existent free of need for a locus, and that is the Essence (dhāt) of God. An existent free of need for a specificator, subsistent in a locus, and that is the attributes of God, because the locus is the Essence. An existent in need of a locus and a specificator, and that is the accident (ʿaraḍ), because it needs to subsist in a body (jirm). An existent free of need for a locus but in need of a specificator, and that is the body (jirm), because it does not need an essence to subsist in but needs an agent, because its bringer into existence is God. And know also that the attributes of God are not said to be in need of the Essence, but rather they are said to subsist in the Essence, because need is only towards another. And it is not said that the attributes are other than the Essence, nor is it said that they are identical to the Essence.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification note (mulāḥaẓa) that divides all existents (al-mawjūdāt) into four categories based on two criteria: need for a locus (mahall) and need for a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The first category is God's Essence (dhāt), which needs neither a locus nor a specificator. The second is God's attributes, which need no specificator (they are eternal) but subsist in a locus (the Essence). The third is accidents (aʿrāḍ), which need both a locus (a body to inhere in) and a specificator that brings them into existence. The fourth is bodies (ajrām, singular jirm), which need a specificator (the agent, God) but do not need a locus, because they subsist in themselves. The author adds an important theological clarification: God's attributes are not said to be 'in need' of the Essence, but rather 'subsist' in it, because 'need' implies dependence on another, which applies only to creatures. Moreover, the attributes are neither identical to the Essence (so as to collapse the distinction) nor fully other (so as to avoid polytheism), maintaining the standard Ash'ari position of distinction without identity.

Key Terms
المحل
المخصص
العرض
الجرم
الصفات
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's attributes are somehow needy of His essence, implying deficiency.
Correction: The passage clarifies that attributes 'subsist' in the Essence, not 'need' it, because need is only towards another. This preserves divine perfection.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true about bodies (jirm) according to the classification?
A) They need a locus but no specificator.
B) They need a specificator but no locus.
C) They need both a locus and a specificator.
D) They need neither a locus nor a specificator.

Key Takeaway
Existing things are divided into four categories based on their need for a locus and a specificator, with God's Essence and attributes having unique positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a body (jirm) that needs a specificator—God—at every moment. Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly turn to the One who brings you into existence? What would change in your day if you truly felt that need right now?
?
The attributes of God are not needy of His essence. They are perfect and eternal. Does your understanding of God's attributes make you feel secure in His perfection, or do you sometimes doubt that His attributes (like mercy or power) are fully realized? How does this lesson challenge those doubts?
?
Accidents like your thoughts, feelings, and movements depend entirely on a locus (your body) and a specificator (God). In what ways do you acknowledge that your very thoughts are gifts from God, not your own production? How can you practice that dependence today?

Permissibility of Describing God with 'Nafs'
Note on the permissibility of 'nafs' for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is theologically permissible to apply the term 'nafs' (self/soul) to Allah, based on explicit Quranic usage.

Core Idea
The Quran itself uses 'nafs' for Allah, proving it is permissible and not anthropomorphic.

This short note addresses a subtle but important point in Islamic theology: whether we can use the word 'nafs' (soul/self) to describe Allah. Some might hesitate because 'nafs' usually refers to the human soul. However, the Quran itself applies this term to Allah in two clear verses. Let's examine the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the note and its Quranic proofs
Read the Arabic passage once to get its full meaning. Then read the translation. Notice that the author first states the permissibility and then provides two verses as evidence.
Pay attention to the context: the verses are from Jesus's speech and a general warning.
2
Reflect on the theological implication
Why is it important that the Quran uses 'nafs' for Allah? This shows that the term, when applied to Allah, does not imply a human-like soul. It is simply a reference to His own self.
This avoids the misconception that 'nafs' always implies a created soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : يجوز وصف الله تعالى بالنفس ، أي إطلاق هذا اللفظ عليه تعالى، قال الله تعالى حاكياً عن نبيه عيسى عليه السلام تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِي وَلَا أَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِكَ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ عَلَّمُ الْغُيُوبِ ) المائدة : ١١٦). وقال تعالى ( وَيُحَذِّرُكُمُ اللَّهُ نَفْسَهُ ﴾ [آل عمران :۲۸].
Note: It is permissible to describe God with 'nafs', i.e., to apply this word to Him. God said, narrating from His prophet Jesus, peace be upon him: 'You know what is in my soul (nafsī) and I do not know what is in Your soul (nafsik), indeed You are the Knower of the unseen.' (5:116). And He said: 'And God warns you of His soul (nafsahu).' (3:28).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This note addresses the theological permissibility of using the Arabic word 'nafs' (often translated as 'self' or 'soul') to refer to God. The author asserts that it is allowed and then provides two Quranic proofs. The first is from Surah al-Ma'ida 5:116, where Jesus says to God: 'You know what is in my soul (nafsī) and I do not know what is in Your soul (nafsik).' The second is from Surah Al 'Imran 3:28: 'And God warns you of His soul (nafsahu).' These verses show that the Quran itself applies 'nafs' to God, thereby legitimizing its use in theological discourse. This is important because some might avoid the term due to its usual connotation with human soul, but the scriptural evidence confirms its applicability to God without implying similarity.

Key Terms
النفس
وصف الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That applying the term 'nafs' to God implies He has a soul like humans.
Correction: The Quranic usage shows it simply refers to God's own self, without anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for allowing the word 'nafs' to describe God?
A) This is always forbidden, so the note is wrong.
B) It is permitted based on consensus.
C) The Quran itself uses 'nafs' for God.
D) It is permitted only in metaphorical exegesis.

Key Takeaway
Describing Allah with 'nafs' is permissible because the Quran itself does so, without implying any similarity to created souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
How does the fact that Allah warns you 'of His nafs' (3:28) affect the seriousness with which you take His commands and prohibitions?
?

Sanusi affirms the permissibility of describing Allah with 'nafs' and cites Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is permissible to describe Allah with 'nafs' and the Quranic evidence for this.

Core Idea
The Quran itself uses 'nafs' for Allah, proving its permissibility.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on describing Allah with the term 'nafs' (self). Although some might object to this term due to anthropomorphic fears, the Quranic usage shows that it is not only permissible but also used by Allah Himself. We will look at the two verses cited by Imam Sanusi.

Read the passage and translation

2

3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Two verses are cited: one from Surah Al-Ma'idah where Jesus speaks, and one from Surah Aal-e-Imran where Allah warns of His 'nafs'.
Both verses use the term 'nafs' in reference to Allah.

Unity of Divine Attributes: Negating Multiplicity
Sanusi negates two types of multiplicity in attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine unity requires each attribute to be singular in essence and that no creature shares God's attributes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are one each, yet all-encompassing; creatures have none like them.

Sanusi now turns to the unity of divine attributes, a corollary of God's oneness. He explains that saying God's attributes are one means two things: there is no multiplicity within God's own attributes, and no creature possesses attributes comparable to God's. This passage clarifies both negations with examples from power, will, and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi presents two types of quantity: continuous and discrete.
2
Understand continuous quantity
Focus on the negation of continuous quantity: God does not have multiple powers, wills, or knowledge. Each attribute is one, yet relates to all relevant objects.
This ensures God's attributes are not multiple in the sense of having more than one instance.
3
Understand discrete quantity
Now focus on discrete quantity: no creature can have attributes like God's (e.g., creative power, specifying will, encompassing knowledge). This maintains God's uniqueness.
The reason given is that God has no equal or likeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدانية الصفات تنفي الكم المتصل والمنفصل فالمتصل أن يكون له قدرتان وإرادتان وهكذا إلى آخر الصفات، بل قدرته واحدة وإرادته واحدة وعلمه واحد (۱) ، ولكن قدرته مع ذلك متعلقة بجميع الممكنات، وعلمه كاشف عن جميع المعلومات، وإرادته مخصصة لجميع الممكنات، وكذا يقال في جميع صفاته، فالتعدد في الصفات محال. وتنفي المنفصل بأن لا يكون لأحد من المخلوقين صفات كصفات الله تعالى، بأن تكون له قدرة توجد وإرادة تخصص وعلم محيط ، وغير ذلك ، وهذا محال لأن الله لا شبيه له.
And unity of Attributes negates continuous and discrete quantity. As for continuous [quantity], it is that He have two powers, two wills, and so on to the rest of the attributes; rather, His power is one, His will is one, and His knowledge is one (1), but His power despite that is related to all possibles, His knowledge discloses all known things, and His will specifies all possibles; and likewise is said regarding all His attributes. So multiplicity in attributes is impossible. And it negates discrete [quantity] in that no creature has attributes like the attributes of Allah, exalted, such that it has a creative power, a specifying will, an encompassing knowledge, and so forth; and this is impossible because Allah has no likeness.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the unity of divine attributes. It states that unity of attributes negates two forms of quantity. First, continuous quantity: this would be if God had multiple powers, multiple wills, etc. Instead, each attribute is singular — one power, one will, one knowledge — yet each is related to all relevant objects (all possibles, all known things). Multiplicity in the attributes themselves is impossible. Second, discrete quantity: no creature may possess attributes like God's, such as a power that creates, a will that specifies, or an encompassing knowledge. This is impossible because God has no equal.

Key Terms
وحدانية الصفات
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because God performs many different acts, He must have many distinct powers or wills.
Correction: Each attribute is one in essence but relates to many things. God's power is one, but it creates all things; His will is one, but it specifies every event.

Checkpoint
What is an example of 'continuous multiplicity' (الكم المتصل) in attributes that is negated?
A) A creature having power like God
B) God having two distinct powers
C) God's knowledge encompassing everything
D) God's will being one

Key Takeaway
Unity of attributes means God has exactly one power, one will, one knowledge, and no creature shares these attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I secretly believe that my own power, will, or knowledge are just smaller versions of God's? How does this lesson shatter that assumption, and what new humility must I cultivate today?
?
When I face a situation beyond my control, do I truly trust that God's single will encompasses every detail? Or do I resent His will because it conflicts with my desires?
?
How often do I act as if my knowledge is sufficient, forgetting that God's one knowledge discloses all things? What step can I take this week to rely more on His knowledge than my own?

The Seven Attributes of Meanings
Enumeration of the seven attributes of meanings and the basis for their count.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of meanings that definitive reason proves for God and how other divine names reduce to these seven.

Core Idea
Reason affirms seven essential divine attributes: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech.

In this lesson, we examine the seven attributes of meanings that form the core of Sunni theology. These are the attributes that rational proof establishes for God, and they serve as the foundation for understanding all other divine names and descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage enumerating the seven attributes carefully. Then read the translation.
Notice the precise terms for each attribute.
2
Identify each attribute
List the seven attributes: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech. Understand what each means in the context of God.
3
Understand the basis for seven
The passage states that definitive evidence only indicates these seven. Reflect on why reason cannot prove additional distinct attributes.
This is a key epistemological point.
4
Examine the examples
Consider the names 'Form-Giver' and 'Avenger'. How do they reduce to the seven? The commentary explains.
This illustrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفات المعاني سبعة وهي القدرة والإرادة والعلم والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام. عد المعاني سبعة لا أكثر، لأن الدليل القطعي إنما دل على هذه السبعة، ولم يدل على أن ما عداها يغايرها، فمثلاً لو قلنا الله تعالى مصور، فإننا لا نفهم من اسم المصور إلا أنه يخلق الصور وكونه تعالى خالق للصور مترتب على كونه تعالى قادراً مريداً عالماً، وهكذا، فالخالق لا بد أن يكون قادراً مريداً عالماً كذلك. والمنتقم كذلك، ولو تمعنت في أكثر الأسماء الحسنى الواردة في الكتاب والسنة لرأيتها ترجع إلى ما ذكرناه، فهذا هو ما نقصده عندما نقول إن الدليل لم يدل على أن الأسماء المذكورة دالة على وجود صفات غير هذه السبعة، وهذا لا يعني حصر صفات الله تعالى في نفس الأمر بهذه الصفات السبعة، ولكن غاية ما نقوله هو أنه لا دليل تفصيليا على الصفات الزائدة تفصيلا، بل الدليل دلّ إجمالاً على أن كمالات الله تعالى غير متناهية، لكن لا تتعلق قدرتنا بمعرفتها تفصيلاً، ولم يرد في الكتاب والسنة ما يدل على ما عدا هذه الصفات دلالة قطعية، والأصل أن نتمسك بالدليل القطعي خصوصا فيما يتعلق بذات الله تعالى وصفاته، وأما ما ورد من نسبة الوجه واليد والعين إلى الله تعالى فراجع إلى ما ذكرناه من الصفات السبعة ولا يزيد عليها.
And the attributes of meanings are seven: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. The attributes of meanings are counted as seven, no more, because definitive evidence only indicates these seven, and it does not indicate that what is beyond them is different from them. For example, if we say that God is 'Form-Giver,' then we understand from the name 'Form-Giver' only that He creates forms, and His being the Creator of forms is consequent upon His being powerful, willing, knowing, and so on. Thus the Creator must be powerful, willing, knowing likewise. And the 'Avenger' is likewise. And if you contemplate most of the beautiful names mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah, you will see that they return to what we have mentioned. This is what we mean when we say that evidence did not indicate that the mentioned names indicate the existence of attributes other than these seven. This does not mean restricting the attributes of God in actual fact to these seven attributes; rather, all we say is that there is no detailed evidence for additional attributes in detail, but evidence indicates in general that God's perfections are infinite, but our power does not extend to knowing them in detail, and nothing in the Book and Sunnah indicates beyond these attributes with definitive evidence. The principle is to adhere to definitive evidence, especially regarding God's essence and attributes. As for what has come regarding the attribution of face, hand, and eye to God, they refer to what we have mentioned of the seven attributes and do not add to them.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage enumerates the seven attributes of meanings (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech) and then argues that definitive evidence does not establish additional distinct attributes beyond these seven. The author gives examples: names like 'Form-Giver' and 'Avenger' ultimately refer to these seven. This does not mean God's perfections are limited; rather, we lack detailed knowledge of further perfections. The passage also clarifies that attributes like face, hand, and eye mentioned in revelation are to be interpreted as referring to these seven attributes.

Key Terms
سبعة
صفات المعاني
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
الحياة
السمع
البصر
الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Other divine names like Form-Giver or Avenger indicate additional distinct attributes beyond the seven.
Correction: The passage argues that such names refer back to the seven attributes and do not imply new ones.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the attributes of meanings counted as seven?
A) Because God's essence only has seven attributes.
B) Because definitive evidence only proves these seven.
C) Because the Quran explicitly limits to seven.
D) Because scholars agreed on seven.

Key Takeaway
Reason definitively affirms seven attributes of God: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech; other names reduce to these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You regularly call upon Allah using His beautiful names like Ar-Rahman and Al-Wadud. Now that you know these names return to the seven attributes, how will this change the way you seek His mercy? Will you consciously connect your plea for mercy to His will and knowledge when you make dua?
?
The passage insists that definitive evidence only proves these seven attributes. Is there any attribute you have been assuming about Allah that might not be independently proven? How does accepting this limitation affect your certainty and humility before Him?
?
If most divine names reduce to these seven attributes, then your understanding of God is meant to be grounded in reason, not imagination. What false image of God might you have carried, and what step will you take today to align your conception with what reason affirms?

Beyond the Seven: Divine Perfections Without Limit
Clarification that God's perfections are infinite and interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's perfections are infinite, and that attributes like face, hand, and eye refer back to the seven attributes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are unlimited, but we only have detailed knowledge of these seven.

Having established the seven rationally proven attributes, the author now clarifies a crucial point: this does not limit God's perfections. In fact, God's perfections are infinite, but we lack detailed evidence for others. Furthermore, anthropomorphic expressions in revelation are interpreted as referring to the seven attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'هذا لا يعني حصر صفات الله تعالى'. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the word 'كمالات' (perfections).
2
Understand the clarification
The author states that limiting to seven is not an actual restriction. Reflect on the difference between what reason proves and what God actually possesses.
This is an important epistemological distinction.
3
Grasp the principle of definitive evidence
The passage emphasizes adhering to definitive evidence, especially for God's essence and attributes. Why is this important?
It prevents speculation.
4
Interpret anthropomorphic expressions
Attributes like face, hand, and eye are said to refer back to the seven attributes. How does this avoid anthropomorphism?
The commentary explains.

Source Text

And the attributes of meanings are seven: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. The Attribute of Perception: Three Positions
Discussion on whether God has an additional attribute of perception beyond the seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological positions on the attribute of perception (idrak), why suspending judgment is considered the most correct, and how the Maturidi view differs from anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Perception in God is debated; suspending judgment is safest.

In this passage, al-Sanusi (via the commentator) outlines the disagreement over whether God has an additional attribute called perception beyond the seven. He presents three views and then clarifies the Maturidi stance to avoid anthropomorphic implications. Let's examine each position and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the three positions and the final Maturidi clarification.
The passage uses technical terms like الإدراك and التوقف.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage presents three views: affirmation, denial, and suspension of judgment. Find each one and understand the reasoning given for each.
The commentator declares tawaqquf as the most correct.
3
Understand why tawaqquf is preferred
The passage argues that while perception is a perfection, its application to God is not revealed, so we neither affirm nor deny. Also the rational proof is weak.
Note the distinction between perfection in temporal vs. eternal beings.
4
Examine the Maturidi stance
The Maturidi Hanafis affirm perception but deny it involves physical contact (mumassa), distinguishing them from anthropomorphists. Perception is like knowledge.
This clarifies that perception does not imply senses in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الخلاف في صفة الإدراك : اختلفوا هل للمولى تعالى صفة زائدة على السبع المعاني تسمى الإدراك يدرك بها الملموسات والمذوقات والمشمومات لأنها كمال وكل كمال يجب أن يثبت له ، أو لا لأنه لم يرد إطلاقه على الله تعالى وصفة العلم مغنية عنه ، وقيل بالتوقف وهو الأصح ، لأنه وإن لم يرد إطلاقه لكنه كمال فلا نثبته ولا ننفيه. والدليل العقلي عليه ضعيف فإنه لا يلزم من كونها كمالا في حق الحادث كونها كمالاً في حق القديم (ص) بتصرف. واعلم أن السادة الماتريدية الأحناف مع قولهم أن الله يدرك المحسوسات وغيرها كما مضى إلا أنهم ينفون أن يكون إدراكه لها بمماسة كما ادعى ذلك بعض المجسمة عليهم. فالإدراك من باب صفة العلم والله تعالى لا يحتاج لكي يعلم إلى مماسة المعلوم فتنبه (س).
Explanation of the disagreement concerning the attribute of perception: They disagreed whether the Exalted Lord has an attribute additional to the seven attributes called perception by which He perceives tangible things, tastes, and smells, because it is a perfection and every perfection must be established for Him, or not, because it has not been reported as applied to God, and the attribute of knowledge suffices for it. And it was said to suspend judgment, and this is the most correct, because even if its application has not been reported, it is a perfection, so we neither affirm nor negate it. And the rational argument for it is weak, for it does not follow that what is a perfection for the temporal is a perfection for the Eternal (modified from al-Sawi). And know that the Maturidi Hanafis, while holding that God perceives sensed things and others as previously mentioned, deny that His perception of them is by contact, as some anthropomorphists claimed against them. Thus perception is of the type of the attribute of knowledge, and God does not need contact with the known in order to know, so pay attention (from the commentator).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological disagreement over whether God has an additional attribute called perception (idrāk) beyond the seven attributes of meanings. Three positions are presented: (1) Affirmation, because it is a perfection and all perfections must be attributed to God. (2) Denial, because the term is not used for God in revelation and the attribute of knowledge suffices. (3) Suspension of judgment (tawaqquf), which is deemed the most correct because although it is a perfection, its application to God is not established. The author also notes that the Maturidi Hanafis affirm that God perceives but deny that this involves physical contact, distinguishing them from anthropomorphists.

Key Terms
الإدراك
الماتريدية
المجسمة
التوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that perception in God implies physical senses or contact, like humans.
Correction: The passage clarifies that perception in God is of the type of knowledge and does not require contact (mumassa). The Maturidi specifically deny contact.

Checkpoint
Which view on the attribute of perception is considered the most correct according to the passage?
A) Affirmation
B) Denial
C) Suspension of judgment
D) Affirmation with contact

Key Takeaway
The debate on perception shows that theological restraint (tawaqquf) is often the safest path when revelation does not clearly affirm or deny an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself wanting to affirm something about God simply because it seems 'perfect' to you, even when no clear text supports it? This passage warns that human notions of perfection do not automatically apply to the Eternal. Where in your belief are you relying on your own reasoning rather than revelation?
?
Suspending judgment (tawaqquf) can feel uncomfortable – it requires admitting you do not know. When you face an ambiguity about Allah, do you rush to take a position, or do you humbly pause? What would it take for you to trust that not knowing can be the safest path?
?
The Maturidis affirm perception but deny contact. Do you ever subconsciously imagine God as having human-like limitations, even if intellectually you reject anthropomorphism? How can you train your heart to think of Allah's knowledge and perception as utterly unlike yours?

The Seven Attributes of Meaning: Limited by Evidence, Not by Reality
Sanusi limits the seven attributes by decisive evidence and clarifies the scope of the limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school limits the attributes of meaning to seven based on decisive evidence, and why this does not confine God's perfection.

Core Idea
The seven attributes are all we can decisively prove, but God's perfection is infinite.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a common question: why are the attributes of meaning only seven? He clarifies that this is not a claim that God has only seven attributes; rather, it is a claim about what we can know with certainty. Let's read his reasoning and see how he uses examples like al-Musawwir to illustrate his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi states his claim and then supports it with an example and clarification.
The passage is dense with theological nuance.
2
Understand the central claim
Identify the main thesis: decisive evidence (الدليل القطعي) only indicates seven attributes of meaning. Anything else is not proven to be a distinct attribute.
This is the basis for the Ash'ari list of seven.
3
Examine the example of al-Musawwir
Sanusi uses the name al-Musawwir to show that the attribute it implies (creating forms) is not separate but a consequence of power, will, and knowledge. Similarly, other names trace back to the seven.
This illustrates how many names do not require new attributes.
4
Grasp the nuance and the principle
Sanusi clarifies that God's perfections are infinite in reality; the limitation is only on what we can prove in detail. The principle is to adhere to decisive evidence, especially regarding God's essence and attributes.
This avoids both denying God's infinite perfection and affirming attributes without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عد المعاني سبعة لا أكثر، لأن الدليل القطعي إنما دل على هذه السبعة، ولم يدل على أن ما عداها يغايرها، فمثلاً لو قلنا الله تعالى ،مصور، فإننا لا نفهم من اسم المصور إلا أنه يخلق الصور وكونه تعالى خالق للصور مترتب على كونه تعالى قادراً مريداً عالماً ، وهكذا، فالخالق لا بد أن يكون قادراً مريداً عالماً كذلك. والمنتقم كذلك، ولو تمعنت في أكثر الأسماء الحسنى الواردة في الكتاب والسنة لرأيتها ترجع إلى ما ذكرناه، فهذا هو ما نقصده عندما نقول إن الدليل لم يدل على أن الأسماء المذكورة دالة على وجود صفات غير هذه السبعة، وهذا لا يعني حصر صفات الله تعالى في نفس الأمر بهذه الصفات السبعة، ولكن غاية ما نقوله هو أنه لا دليل تفصيليا على الصفات الزائدة تفصيلا، بل الدليل دلّ إجمالاً على أن كمالات الله تعالى غير متناهية، لكن لا تتعلق قدرتنا بمعرفتها تفصيلاً، ولم يرد في الكتاب والسنة ما يدل على ما عدا هذه الصفات دلالة قطعية، والأصل أن نتمسك بالدليل القطعي خصوصا فيما يتعلق بذات الله تعالى وصفاته
The meanings are seven, not more, because decisive evidence only indicated these seven, and did not indicate that what is other than them is distinct from them. For example, if we say 'God is the Fashioner', we only understand from the name 'al-Musawwir' that He creates forms, and that His being the Creator of forms is consequent upon His being Powerful, Willing, Knowing, and so on. Thus, the Creator must necessarily be Powerful, Willing, and Knowing likewise. Similarly with 'al-Muntaqim'. If you ponder most of the Beautiful Names mentioned in the Book and the Sunnah, you will see that they revert to what we have mentioned. This is what we mean when we say that the evidence did not indicate that the mentioned names indicate the existence of attributes other than these seven. This does not mean confining God's attributes in themselves to these seven attributes, but the utmost we say is that there is no detailed evidence for additional attributes in detail. Rather, the evidence indicated in general that God's perfections are infinite, but our power does not extend to knowing them in detail, and nothing has come in the Book and Sunnah that indicates what is other than these attributes decisively. The principle is to adhere to decisive evidence, especially regarding God's essence and attributes.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and defense of the standard Ash'ari position that the attributes of meaning are only seven: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. The author argues that decisive evidence (الدليل القطعي) only proves these seven; for any other attribute, there is no decisive evidence that it is a distinct attribute from these. He illustrates with the name 'al-Musawwir' (the Fashioner): the attribute implied by this name is not a separate attribute but rather a consequence of God being powerful, willful, and knowing. The same applies to names like 'al-Muntaqim' (the Avenger). Most of the Beautiful Names, when examined, trace back to these seven attributes. The author then clarifies a key nuance: this does not mean that God's attributes are limited to seven in reality (في نفس الأمر); rather, it means that we have no detailed (تفصيلي) evidence for additional attributes. There is general evidence that God's perfections are infinite, but our intellect cannot know them in detail, and revelation does not decisively indicate any specific additional attributes. The principle is to rely on decisive evidence, especially concerning God's essence and attributes.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
الأسماء الحسنى
الدليل القطعي
تفصيلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that limiting the seven attributes means God has only seven attributes in reality.
Correction: The author clarifies that this limitation is only about what we can prove with decisive evidence; God's perfections are infinite but beyond our detailed knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for limiting the attributes of meaning to seven?
A) Because God only has seven attributes.
B) Because only seven are mentioned in the Quran.
C) Because decisive evidence only indicates these seven.
D) Because the early scholars agreed on seven.

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of meaning are affirmed because decisive evidence proves them, but God's perfections are infinite and beyond our detailed knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by one of His names, are you satisfied with merely knowing it traces to one of the seven attributes, or do you seek to truly connect with that attribute in your worship? What if you are content with intellectual classification without transformative devotion?
?
This lesson says God's perfections are infinite beyond our detailed knowledge. Have you ever caught yourself trying to confine Allah to what you can understand, as if your mind can encompass Him? What idol of 'mental certainty' do you need to dismantle today?
?
The author emphasizes adhering to decisive evidence regarding God's attributes. Where in your life do you flirt with speculation about Allah instead of submitting to what has been decisively revealed? How will you repent from that today?

The Three Opinions on the Attribute of Perception
Sanusi presents the three opinions on whether God has an additional attribute of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on whether God has an additional attribute of perception (al-idrāk) and why the author considers abstention the most correct view.

Core Idea
Abstaining from affirming or denying perception safeguards theological caution.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a theological dispute about whether God has a separate attribute called 'perception' that allows Him to perceive tangibles, tastes, and smells. He outlines three positions—affirmation, denial, and abstention—and explains why abstention is the most prudent stance. He also clarifies that the Maturidi school, while affirming perception, rejects the anthropomorphic notion of physical contact. This lesson will help you navigate this nuanced discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three positions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Look for the three opinions: affirmation (يثبت), denial (ينفي), and abstention (توقف). Notice how each is supported.
The abstention is described as 'الأصح' (most correct).
2
Understand why abstention is preferred
Focus on the reasoning: perception is a perfection for contingent beings, but that does not automatically make it a perfection for the Eternal. The rational proof is weak, and the term is not reported in revelation.
3
Note the Maturidi clarification
The Maturidis affirm perception but deny that it requires physical contact (مماسة). Perception is akin to knowledge; God knows without touching.
This counters the accusation by some anthropomorphists.
4
Reflect on the principle of perfection
Consider the broader rule: not every perfection in creation is necessarily a perfection for God. This is key in divine attribute debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الخلاف في صفة الإدراك : اختلفوا هل للمولى تعالى صفة زائدة على السبع المعاني تسمى الإدراك يدرك بها الملموسات والمذوقات والمشمومات لأنها كمال وكل كمال يجب أن يثبت له ، أو لا لأنه لم يرد إطلاقه على الله تعالى وصفة العلم مغنية عنه ، وقيل بالتوقف وهو الأصح ، لأنه وإن لم يرد إطلاقه لكنه كمال فلا نثبته ولا ننفيه. والدليل العقلي عليه ضعيف فإنه لا يلزم من كونها كمالا في حق الحادث كونها كمالاً في حق القديم (ص) بتصرف. واعلم أن السادة الماتريدية الأحناف مع قولهم أن الله يدرك المحسوسات وغيرها كما مضى إلا أنهم ينفون أن يكون إدراكه لها بمماسة كما ادعى ذلك بعض المجسمة عليهم. فالإدراك من باب صفة العلم والله تعالى لا يحتاج لكي يعلم إلى مماسة المعلوم فتنبه (س).
Explanation of the disagreement concerning the attribute of perception: They differed: is there for God, exalted, an additional attribute beyond the seven meanings called perception by which He perceives tangible things, tastes, and smells because it is a perfection and every perfection must be affirmed for Him? Or not because its application to God has not been reported and the attribute of knowledge suffices for it? And it was said to abstain, and that is the most correct, because even though its application has not been reported, it is a perfection, so we neither affirm it nor deny it. The rational proof for it is weak, for it does not follow from its being a perfection with respect to the contingent that it is a perfection with respect to the Eternal. (ص) with adaptation. And know that the Maturidi Hanafi masters, despite their statement that God perceives sensible things and others as mentioned previously, they deny that His perception of them is by contact as some of the anthropomorphists claimed against them. So perception is of the category of the attribute of knowledge, and God does not need to know something by contact with the known. So be alert. (س).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and evaluation of the differing opinions on whether God has an additional attribute called 'perception' (الإدراك) beyond the seven known attributes. Three positions are described: (1) Affirmation: because perception is a perfection, and every perfection must be attributed to God. (2) Denial: because the term is not applied to God in revelation, and the attribute of knowledge is sufficient for perceiving things. (3) Abstention (التوقف): which the author declares the most correct (الأصح). The reason is that while the attribute is indeed a perfection in contingent beings, it does not necessarily follow that it is a perfection in the Eternal, and rational proof is weak. The author then notes that the Maturidi Hanafis affirm that God perceives sensibles but they reject the idea that perception involves physical contact (مماسة), which some anthropomorphists (مجسمة) attribute to them. Instead, perception is related to knowledge; God knows without contact. The lesson ends with a warning to be attentive to this nuance.

Key Terms
الإدراك
التوقف
المجسمة
الماتريدية
صفة العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that abstaining from affirming perception contradicts the principle that God possesses all perfections.
Correction: Abstention is not a denial of perfection; it is a recognition that not every perfection in contingent beings is necessarily a perfection for God, and due to lack of decisive evidence, we remain silent.

Checkpoint
What is the author's preferred opinion regarding the attribute of perception?
A) Affirm it as a separate attribute.
B) Deny it entirely.
C) Abstain from affirming or denying.
D) Affirm it only in relation to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The most correct position regarding the attribute of perception is abstention, because rational proof is weak and revelation does not apply the term, even though it is a perfection in contingent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you affirm or deny matters of the unseen without clear evidence, simply because your community does? Could your 'caution' be a form of cowardice in seeking truth?
?
The author says abstention is safest because rational proof is weak. When was the last time you admitted you didn't know something about God and left it unspoken? Are you comfortable with ignorance in matters of faith?
?
Compare your tendency to adopt a position on every theological detail versus the humility of 'I do not know.' Which path leads closer to Allah?

Definition and Oneness of Divine Power
Sanusi defines divine power and proves its oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine power as an eternal attribute and the rational proof that it must be one.

Core Idea
Divine power is one because multiple powers would produce two effectors on one effect.

Sanusi begins by defining the divine attribute of power with precision. He then immediately establishes its oneness through a clear rational proof. This foundational concept is essential for all further discussions about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the definition of power as an eternal attribute. Then read the translation.
The definition includes key terms: 'eternal', 'creation and annihilation', 'in accordance with will'.
2
Identify the proof of oneness
The passage states that power must be one, and provides a proof. Find the proof and understand its logic: multiple powers would lead to two effectors on a single effect, which is impossible.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Consider why the impossibility of two effectors on one effect is a valid proof. Why would that be impossible?
This connects to the principle of rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة القدرة : صفة أزلية يتأتى بها إيجاد كل ممكن وإعدامه على وفق الإرادة (٢). يجب أن تثبت للقدرة أنها واحدة ودليل هذا أنه يلزم على تعددها اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد. ويجب أن تثبت أنها عامة التعلق بالممكنات ودليل هذا أنه لو خرج ممكن عن تعلقها لزم منه العجز، والحاصل أن تعلق القدرة التنجيزي مرتب في التعلق والحصول على تعلق الإرادة التنجيزي، وتعلق الإرادة مرتب على تعلق العلم في التعلق أيضاً لا في الوجود، لأن الواقع أنهما قديمان. وهذا معنى قولهم يجب الإيمان بالقضاء والقدر، فالقضاء هو عين تعلق الإرادة والعلم التنجيزيين، والقدر هو عين تعلق القدرة التنجيزي وتعلق الإرادة التنجيزي الحادث على القول به.(ص) إمكان الصلوحي متفق عليه ولم يخالف فيه إلا بعض شواذ المعتزلة والتمانع إنما يتم مع فرض التنجيزي لا الصلوحي أي يتم بفرض التعلق التنجيزي ولا يسترط تعلقه بالفعل ومجرد تحقق الصلوحي كاف لفرض التنجيزي . (س) . وجهة ورود العجز عند عدم التعلق التنجيزي هي أنها صالحة ولكنها لم تتعلق بالفعل ، فالسبب في عدم تعلقها إما من الذات أو غير الذات، ومهما كان فيلزم انفعالها وتخصيصها ، مع لزوم الدور أو التسلسل في الاحتمال الأول والعجز القطعي من الاحتمال الثاني . (٢) أي إن إيجاد الله للممكن وإعدامه له على وفق تعلق إرادته به لأنه تعالى لا يوجد أو يعدم بقدرته إلا ما أراد وجوده أو عدمه من الممكنات، وبهذا تعلم أن تعلق القدرة فرع عن تعلق الإرادة أي تابع له ومتأخر عنه في التعقل . (غ) فالتابعية هذه ليست زمانية كما قد يتوهم البعض، بل هي فقط في التعقل، بمعنى أن العقل لا يدرك قادراً إلا مريداً، فالتصديق بالإرادة شرط عقلي للتصديق بالقدرة.(س).
And the reality of power: an eternal attribute by which is effected the creation of every possible thing and its annihilation in accordance with will (2). It must be affirmed that power is one, and the proof of this is that if it were multiple, there would be the concurrence of two effectors upon a single effect. And it must be affirmed that it has universal connection to possible things, and the proof of this is that if a possible thing were outside its connection, then impotence would necessarily follow. And the summary is that the effective connection (التعلق التنجيزي) of power is subordinate in connection and occurrence to the effective connection of will, and the connection of will is subordinate to the connection of knowledge in connection, not in existence, because in reality both are eternal. And this is the meaning of their statement, 'It is necessary to believe in al-qada' and al-qadar': al-qada' is the very effective connection of will and knowledge, and al-qadar is the very effective connection of power and the temporal effective connection of will according to those who affirm it. The possibility of fitness (إمكان الصلوحي) is agreed upon, and none opposed it except some deviant Mu'tazila. And the mutual prevention (التمانع) is only realized with the assumption of the effective connection, not the fitness connection; that is, it is realized by assuming the effective connection, and its connection to the act is not required; merely the realization of fitness is sufficient for assuming the effective connection. (س). The aspect of the occurrence of impotence when there is no effective connection is that it (the power) is fit but did not connect to the act. So the reason for its non-connection is either from the essence or other than the essence, and whatever it may be, its passivity and specification necessarily result, along with the necessity of circularity or infinite regress in the first possibility and absolute impotence in the second possibility. (2) That is, God's bringing into existence and annihilating a possible thing is in accordance with His will's connection to it, because He does not bring into existence or annihilate by His power except what He willed to exist or not exist of possible things. By this you know that the connection of power is a branch of the connection of will, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in mental consideration (غ). So this dependence is not temporal as some might suppose, but rather only in mental consideration, in the sense that the intellect does not perceive a powerful being except as a willing being, so affirming will is a rational condition for affirming power. (س).
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine attribute of power (القدرة). It begins with the definition: power is an eternal attribute by which God creates and annihilates every possible thing in accordance with His will. The author then makes two key points about power. First, power must be one; if there were multiple powers, two effectors would act on a single effect, leading to impossibility. Second, power must have universal connection to all possible things; if any possible thing were outside its scope, that would imply impotence (العجز). The author explains the relationship between power, will, and knowledge: the effective connection of power is subordinate to the effective connection of will, and will is subordinate to knowledge in mental consideration (in order of understanding), not in time, because both power and will are eternal. This is linked to the concepts of al-qada' and al-qadar: al-qada' is the effective connection of will and knowledge, and al-qadar is the effective connection of power. The passage then discusses the concept of 'fitness' (الصلوحي) and 'effective' (التنجيزي) connections. The possibility of fitness is agreed upon, but the problem of mutual prevention (تمانع) only arises with effective connection. Finally, the author explains why the absence of effective connection would lead to impotence, and he refutes any misunderstanding that the dependence of power on will is temporal; it is only a logical priority in the mind—one cannot conceive of a powerful agent without also conceiving of it as willing.

Key Terms
القدرة
الصفة الأزلية
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the proof that divine power is one?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because if there were multiple powers, there would be two effectors on one effect.
C) Because it is universal.
D) Because it is in accordance with will.

Key Takeaway
Divine power is one, and its oneness is proven by the impossibility of two effectors on one effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's power is a single, undivided attribute that never wavers or splits, how does that change the way you view His ability to answer your prayers—especially when you pray for many different things at once? Will you pray with more confidence or more fear? Why?
?
The proof of oneness shows that God's power is not like human power, which can be distracted or divided. In what areas of your life have you been acting as if God's attention or power is limited—as if He cannot handle all your needs at the same time? What specific thought or action will you change today to reflect the truth that His power is one and sufficient?
?
The same single power creates and annihilates everything. How attached are you to the things of this world that will one day be annihilated by that same power? If you truly internalized this lesson, what is one possession, relationship, or ambition you would immediately loosen your grip on? What would it look like to entrust it fully to the One whose power is one?

Divine Attributes

The Universal Scope of Divine Power and Its Relationship to Will and Knowledge
Sanusi proves the universal scope of power and explains its relation to will and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine power extends to all possible things and that its effective connection is rationally dependent on will and knowledge.

Core Idea
Power universally connects to all possibles but is rationally subordinate to will and knowledge.

Sanusi now argues that divine power must have universal connection to all possible things, otherwise impotence would result. He then clarifies the hierarchical relationship between power, will, and knowledge. This lesson also explains the meaning of al-qada' and al-qadar in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for universal connection
Read the passage that begins with 'ويجب أن تثبت أنها عامة التعلق'. Understand the proof: if a possible thing were outside power's scope, impotence would follow.
2
Note the subordination of power to will
The passage states that the effective connection of power is subordinate to the effective connection of will. Also, will is subordinate to knowledge in mental consideration, not in time.
This subordination is rational, not temporal.
3
Learn the definitions of qada and qadar
Al-qada' is the effective connection of will and knowledge; al-qadar is the effective connection of power and the temporal will.
This explains the common phrase 'belief in qada and qadar'.
4
Understand the rational condition
The passage ends by stating that affirming will is a rational condition for affirming power. Think about why that makes sense.

Divine power is universal, and its effective exercise is rationally dependent on will and knowledge, but all are eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God's power reaches every possible thing. But when you face a situation you deem impossible—like healing from a terminal illness or being forgiven after a grave sin—does your heart truly expect His power to act? Or do you subtly limit His power by your despair? Today, identify one 'impossible' thing you will consciously entrust to His universal power, and do it.
?
This lesson teaches that power is rationally subordinate to will and knowledge. Your own actions: are they driven by blind impulse (like a power without direction) or are they guided by knowledge and deliberate choice? Examine your habits—how often do you act without truly knowing why, without pausing to align your will with what God knows is best? Commit to one hour of reflection this week to reorient your will toward knowledge of His will.
?
Al-Qada’ and al-Qadar are cosmic truths. But do you live as if your every moment is under God’s effective decree? Or do you compartmentalize—accepting qadar for 'big things' but worrying over 'small' details as if they escape His will? Pick one anxiety you carry today and say out loud: 'This is part of al-qada’ and al-qadar—God’s will and power are effectively connected to it.' Then surrender it.

Fitness vs. Effective Connection and the Impossibility of Impotence
Sanusi distinguishes fitness and effective connection and argues the necessity of effective connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the fitness connection and the effective connection of power, and why the absence of effective connection would imply impotence.

Core Idea
Fitness is always assumed, but impotence only follows from lack of effective connection, not from lack of fitness.

Sanusi addresses a more technical point: the difference between power having the fitness to act and actually acting (effective connection). He explains that the possibility of impotence only arises when the effective connection is absent, not when only fitness exists. He then provides a detailed argument showing that the absence of effective connection leads to either circularity or absolute impotence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terms: fitness vs effective
Read the passage carefully. 'الصلوحي' refers to the fitness of power to act, while 'التنجيزي' refers to the actual effective connection. Note that fitness is agreed upon except by some deviant Mu'tazila.
2
Note the scope of mutual prevention
The passage states that mutual prevention (التمانع) only applies to effective connection, not fitness. This is important in arguments for God's oneness.
3
Follow the argument for impotence
The author explains that if effective connection is absent despite fitness, the reason is either from the essence or from external. Both lead to unacceptable consequences: circularity/infinite regress or absolute impotence.
Try to trace the logical chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة القدرة : صفة أزلية يتأتى بها إيجاد كل ممكن وإعدامه على وفق الإرادة (٢). يجب أن تثبت للقدرة أنها واحدة ودليل هذا أنه يلزم على تعددها اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد. ويجب أن تثبت أنها عامة التعلق بالممكنات ودليل هذا أنه لو خرج ممكن عن تعلقها لزم منه العجز، والحاصل أن تعلق القدرة التنجيزي مرتب في التعلق والحصول على تعلق الإرادة التنجيزي، وتعلق الإرادة مرتب على تعلق العلم في التعلق أيضاً لا في الوجود، لأن الواقع أنهما قديمان. وهذا معنى قولهم يجب الإيمان بالقضاء والقدر، فالقضاء هو عين تعلق الإرادة والعلم التنجيزيين، والقدر هو عين تعلق القدرة التنجيزي وتعلق الإرادة التنجيزي الحادث على القول به.(ص) إمكان الصلوحي متفق عليه ولم يخالف فيه إلا بعض شواذ المعتزلة والتمانع إنما يتم مع فرض التنجيزي لا الصلوحي أي يتم بفرض التعلق التنجيزي ولا يسترط تعلقه بالفعل ومجرد تحقق الصلوحي كاف لفرض التنجيزي . (س) . وجهة ورود العجز عند عدم التعلق التنجيزي هي أنها صالحة ولكنها لم تتعلق بالفعل ، فالسبب في عدم تعلقها إما من الذات أو غير الذات، ومهما كان فيلزم انفعالها وتخصيصها ، مع لزوم الدور أو التسلسل في الاحتمال الأول والعجز القطعي من الاحتمال الثاني . (٢) أي إن إيجاد الله للممكن وإعدامه له على وفق تعلق إرادته به لأنه تعالى لا يوجد أو يعدم بقدرته إلا ما أراد وجوده أو عدمه من الممكنات، وبهذا تعلم أن تعلق القدرة فرع عن تعلق الإرادة أي تابع له ومتأخر عنه في التعقل . (غ) فالتابعية هذه ليست زمانية كما قد يتوهم البعض، بل هي فقط في التعقل، بمعنى أن العقل لا يدرك قادراً إلا مريداً، فالتصديق بالإرادة شرط عقلي للتصديق بالقدرة.(س).
And the reality of power: an eternal attribute by which is effected the creation of every possible thing and its annihilation in accordance with will (2). It must be affirmed that power is one, and the proof of this is that if it were multiple, there would be the concurrence of two effectors upon a single effect. And it must be affirmed that it has universal connection to possible things, and the proof of this is that if a possible thing were outside its connection, then impotence would necessarily follow. And the summary is that the effective connection (التعلق التنجيزي) of power is subordinate in connection and occurrence to the effective connection of will, and the connection of will is subordinate to the connection of knowledge in connection, not in existence, because in reality both are eternal. And this is the meaning of their statement, 'It is necessary to believe in al-qada' and al-qadar': al-qada' is the very effective connection of will and knowledge, and al-qadar is the very effective connection of power and the temporal effective connection of will according to those who affirm it. The possibility of fitness (إمكان الصلوحي) is agreed upon, and none opposed it except some deviant Mu'tazila. And the mutual prevention (التمانع) is only realized with the assumption of the effective connection, not the fitness connection; that is, it is realized by assuming the effective connection, and its connection to the act is not required; merely the realization of fitness is sufficient for assuming the effective connection. (س). The aspect of the occurrence of impotence when there is no effective connection is that it (the power) is fit but did not connect to the act. So the reason for its non-connection is either from the essence or other than the essence, and whatever it may be, its passivity and specification necessarily result, along with the necessity of circularity or infinite regress in the first possibility and absolute impotence in the second possibility. (2) That is, God's bringing into existence and annihilating a possible thing is in accordance with His will's connection to it, because He does not bring into existence or annihilate by His power except what He willed to exist or not exist of possible things. By this you know that the connection of power is a branch of the connection of will, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in mental consideration (غ). So this dependence is not temporal as some might suppose, but rather only in mental consideration, in the sense that the intellect does not perceive a powerful being except as a willing being, so affirming will is a rational condition for affirming power. (س).
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine attribute of power (القدرة). It begins with the definition: power is an eternal attribute by which God creates and annihilates every possible thing in accordance with His will. The author then makes two key points about power. First, power must be one; if there were multiple powers, two effectors would act on a single effect, leading to impossibility. Second, power must have universal connection to all possible things; if any possible thing were outside its scope, that would imply impotence (العجز). The author explains the relationship between power, will, and knowledge: the effective connection of power is subordinate to the effective connection of will, and will is subordinate to knowledge in mental consideration (in order of understanding), not in time, because both power and will are eternal. This is linked to the concepts of al-qada' and al-qadar: al-qada' is the effective connection of will and knowledge, and al-qadar is the effective connection of power. The passage then discusses the concept of 'fitness' (الصلوحي) and 'effective' (التنجيزي) connections. The possibility of fitness is agreed upon, but the problem of mutual prevention (تمانع) only arises with effective connection. Finally, the author explains why the absence of effective connection would lead to impotence, and he refutes any misunderstanding that the dependence of power on will is temporal; it is only a logical priority in the mind—one cannot conceive of a powerful agent without also conceiving of it as willing.

التنجيزي
التمانع
العجز
